<p><b>Niger</b> or <b>the Niger</b><sup id="cite_ref-nameISO_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nameISO-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nameUNGEGN_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nameUNGEGN-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ʒ/: &#39;s&#39; in &#39;pleasure&#39;">ʒ</span><span title="/ɛər/: &#39;are&#39; in &#39;bare&#39;">ɛər</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/aɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">aɪ</span><span title="/dʒ/: &#39;j&#39; in &#39;jam&#39;">dʒ</span><span title="/ər/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <small>French:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[niʒɛʁ]</a></span>), officially the <b>Republic of the Niger</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-nameISO_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nameISO-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nameUNGEGN_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nameUNGEGN-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> named after the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a>. Niger is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> to the northeast, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a> to the southwest, <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a> to the west, and <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> to the northwest. Niger covers a land area of almost 1,270,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (490,000&#160;sq&#160;mi), making it the largest country in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>. Over 80% of its land area lies in the <a href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara Desert</a>. The country's predominantly <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Niger" title="Islam in Niger">Islamic</a> population of about 21 million<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> live mostly in clusters in the far south and west of the country. The capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Niamey" title="Niamey">Niamey</a>, located in Niger's southwest corner.
</p><p>Niger is a <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing country</a>, which consistently ranks near the bottom in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>' <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> (HDI); it was ranked 187th of 188 countries for 2015 and 189th out of 189 countries in the 2018 report.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Many of the non-desert portions of the country are threatened by periodic drought and <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>. The economy is concentrated around <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_agriculture" title="Subsistence agriculture">subsistence</a>, with some export agriculture in the more fertile south, and export of raw materials, especially <a href="/wiki/Uranium_ore" title="Uranium ore">uranium ore</a>. Niger faces serious challenges to development due to its landlocked position, desert terrain, inefficient <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Niger" title="Agriculture in Niger">agriculture</a>, high <a href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertility rate">fertility rates</a> without <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a>, and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Overpopulation" title="Overpopulation">overpopulation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-EconPop_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconPop-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> the poor educational level and poverty of its people, lack of infrastructure, poor healthcare, and environmental degradation.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Niger" title="Demographics of Niger">Nigerien society</a> reflects a diversity drawn from the long independent histories of its several ethnic groups and regions and their relatively short period living in a single state. Historically, what is now Niger has been on the fringes of several large states. Since independence, Nigeriens have lived under five constitutions and three periods of <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">military rule</a>. After the military coup in 2010, Niger became a democratic, multi-party state. A majority of the population lives in rural areas, and has little access to advanced education.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Niger" title="History of Niger">History of Niger</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1997_278-10_Sahara_glyph.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/1997_278-10_Sahara_glyph.jpg/220px-1997_278-10_Sahara_glyph.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/1997_278-10_Sahara_glyph.jpg/330px-1997_278-10_Sahara_glyph.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/1997_278-10_Sahara_glyph.jpg/440px-1997_278-10_Sahara_glyph.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1680" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1997_278-10_Sahara_glyph.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient rock engraving showing herds of giraffe, ibex, and other animals in the southern Sahara near Tiguidit, Niger.</div></div></div>
<p>Early human settlement in Niger is evidenced by numerous archaeological remains. In prehistoric times, the climate of the Sahara (Ténéré desert in Niger) was wet and provided favorable conditions for agriculture and livestock herding in a fertile grassland environment five thousand years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005–06, a graveyard in the Ténéré desert was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Sereno" title="Paul Sereno">Paul Sereno</a>, a paleontologist from the University of Chicago.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> His team discovered 5,000-year-old remains of a woman and two children in the Ténéré Desert.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The evidence along with remains of animals that do not typically live in desert are among the strongest evidence of the 'green' Sahara in Niger. It is believed that progressive desertification around 5000 BC pushed sedentary populations to the south and south-east (Lake Chad).<sup id="cite_ref-larousse-history_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-larousse-history-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photo1906_Zinder_overview.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Photo1906_Zinder_overview.jpg/220px-Photo1906_Zinder_overview.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Photo1906_Zinder_overview.jpg/330px-Photo1906_Zinder_overview.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Photo1906_Zinder_overview.jpg/440px-Photo1906_Zinder_overview.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photo1906_Zinder_overview.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overlooking the town of <a href="/wiki/Zinder" title="Zinder">Zinder</a> and the Sultan's Palace from the French fort (1906). The arrival of the French spelled a sudden end for precolonial states like the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Damagaram" title="Sultanate of Damagaram">Sultanate of Damagaram</a>, which carried on only as ceremonial "chiefs" appointed by the colonial government.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Empires_and_kingdoms_in_pre-colonial_Niger">Empires and kingdoms in pre-colonial Niger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Empires and kingdoms in pre-colonial Niger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By at least the 5th century BC, Niger had become an area of trans-Saharan trade, led by the Berber tribes from the north, who used camels as a well-adapted means of transportation through the desert. This trade made <a href="/wiki/Agadez" title="Agadez">Agadez</a> a pivotal place of the trans-Saharan trade. This mobility, which would continue in waves for several centuries, was accompanied with further migration to the south and intermixing between sub-Saharan African and North African populations. It was also aided by the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> to the region at the end of the 7th century.<sup id="cite_ref-larousse-history_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-larousse-history-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Several empires and kingdoms also flourished during this era, up to the beginning of colonization in Africa.
</p>
<h4><span id="Songhai_Empire_.28600.E2.80.931591.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Songhai_Empire_(600–1591)">Songhai Empire (600–1591)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Songhai Empire (600–1591)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai Empire</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai Empire</a> was an empire bearing the name of its main ethnic group, the <a href="/wiki/Songhai_people" title="Songhai people">Songhai or Sonrai</a>, located in western Africa on the bend of the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a> in present-day Niger, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a>. In the 7th century, Songhai tribes settled down north of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Niamey" title="Niamey">Niamey</a> and founded the Songhai city-states of Koukia and <a href="/wiki/Gao" title="Gao">Gao</a>. By the 11th century, Gao had become the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-boubou-hama_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boubou-hama-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1000 to 1325, The Songhai Empire prospered and managed to maintain peace with its neighboring empires including the <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a>. In 1325 the Songhai Empire was conquered by the Mali Empire but was freed in 1335 by prince Ali Kolen and his brother, Songhai princes held captive by <a href="/wiki/Musa_I_of_Mali" title="Musa I of Mali">Moussa Kankan</a>, the ruler of the Mali Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-boubou-hama_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boubou-hama-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> From the mid-15th to the late 16th century, Songhai was one of the largest Islamic empires in history.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaouartal-bei-Bilma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Kaouartal-bei-Bilma.jpg/220px-Kaouartal-bei-Bilma.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Kaouartal-bei-Bilma.jpg/330px-Kaouartal-bei-Bilma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Kaouartal-bei-Bilma.jpg/440px-Kaouartal-bei-Bilma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="398" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaouartal-bei-Bilma.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kaouar" title="Kaouar">Kaouar</a> escarpment, forming an oasis in the <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A9n%C3%A9r%C3%A9" title="Ténéré">Ténéré</a> desert.</div></div></div>
<h4><span id="Hausa_kingdoms_.28mid-14th_century_.E2.80.93_1808.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hausa_kingdoms_(mid-14th_century_–_1808)">Hausa kingdoms (mid-14th century – 1808)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hausa kingdoms (mid-14th century – 1808)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hausa_Kingdoms" title="Hausa Kingdoms">Hausa Kingdoms</a></div>
<p>Between the Niger River and Lake Chad lay <a href="/wiki/Hausa_Kingdoms" title="Hausa Kingdoms">Hausa</a> kingdoms and fertile areas. These kingdoms flourished from the mid-14th century up until the early 19th century, when they were conquered by <a href="/wiki/Usman_dan_Fodio" title="Usman dan Fodio">Usman dan Fodio</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sokoto Empire">Sokoto Empire</a>. The Hausa kingdoms were not a compact entity but several federations of kingdoms more or less independent of one other. Their organization was somewhat democratic: the Hausa kings were elected by the notables of the country and could be removed by them.<sup id="cite_ref-boubou-hama_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boubou-hama-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Hausa Kingdoms began as seven states founded according to the <a href="/wiki/Bayajidda" title="Bayajidda">Bayajidda</a> legend by the six sons of Bawo. Bawo was the only son of the Hausa queen <a href="/wiki/Daurama" title="Daurama">Daurama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bayajidda" title="Bayajidda">Bayajidda</a> or (<a href="/wiki/Abu_Yazid" title="Abu Yazid">Abu Yazid</a> according to certain Nigerien historians) who came from <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>. The seven original Hausa states were: Daoura (state of queen <a href="/wiki/Daurama" title="Daurama">Daurama</a>), Kano, Rano, Zaria, Gobir, Katsena and Biram.<sup id="cite_ref-boubou-hama_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boubou-hama-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mali_Empire">Mali Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mali Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a></div>
<p>The Mali Empire was a <a href="/wiki/Mandinka_people" title="Mandinka people">Mandinka</a> empire founded by <a href="/wiki/Sundiata_Keita" title="Sundiata Keita">Sundiata Keita</a> <i>circa</i> 1230 that existed up to 1600. At its peak circa 1350, the empire extended as far west as Senegal and Guinee Conakry and as far east as western Niger.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kanem-Bornu_Empire">Kanem-Bornu Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Kanem-Bornu Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kanem-Bornu_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanem-Bornu Empire">Kanem-Bornu Empire</a></div>
<p>The Kanem-Bornu Empire was an empire that existed in modern-day Chad, Nigeria, Cameroon, Niger and Libya. The empire first existed and prospered as the <a href="/wiki/Kanem_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanem Empire">Kanem Empire</a> as early as the 9th century and later as the Kingdom of Bornu until 1900.
</p>
<h3><span id="French_Niger_.281900.E2.80.9358.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Niger_(1900–58)">French Niger (1900–58)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: French Niger (1900–58)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Senegambia_and_Niger" title="Senegambia and Niger">Senegambia and Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Upper_Senegal_and_Niger" title="Upper Senegal and Niger">Upper Senegal and Niger</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Niger" title="Colony of Niger">Colony of Niger</a></div>
<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2016</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:French_West_Africa_1913_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/French_West_Africa_1913_map.png/220px-French_West_Africa_1913_map.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/French_West_Africa_1913_map.png/330px-French_West_Africa_1913_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/French_West_Africa_1913_map.png 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="363" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:French_West_Africa_1913_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a>, circa 1913</div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th century, contact with Europe began with the first European explorers—notably Monteil (French) and Barth (German)—to travel to Niger.
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">1885 Berlin conference</a> during which colonial powers outlined the division of Africa into colonial spheres, French military efforts to conquer existing African states were intensified in all French colonies including Niger. This included several military expeditions including the <a href="/wiki/Voulet%E2%80%93Chanoine_Mission" title="Voulet–Chanoine Mission">Voulet Chanoine Mission</a>, which became notorious for pillaging, looting, raping and killing many local civilians on its passage. On 8 May 1899, in retaliation for the resistance of queen <a href="/wiki/Sarraounia" title="Sarraounia">Sarraounia</a>, captain Voulet and his men murdered all the inhabitants of the village of Birni-N'Konni in what is regarded as one of the worst massacres in French colonial history. French military expeditions met great resistance from several ethnic groups, especially Hausa and Tuareg groups. The most notable Tuareg revolt was the <a href="/wiki/Kaocen_Revolt" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaocen Revolt">Kaocen Revolt</a>. The French authorities also abolished the widespread slavery among Tuareg communities.
</p><p>By 1922, all resistance to colonial rule was eliminated and <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Niger" title="Colony of Niger">Niger</a> became a French colony. Niger's colonial history and development parallel that of other French West African territories. France administered her West African colonies through a governor general at Dakar, Senegal, and governors in the individual territories, including Niger. In addition to conferring a limited form of French citizenship on the inhabitants of the territories, the 1946 French constitution provided for decentralization of power and limited participation in political life for local advisory assemblies.
</p><p>The end of the colonial era was characterized by a transformation of the political environment in French West Africa and Niger. The Nigerien Progressive Party, the Nigerien section of the African Democratic Rally Party, founded in May 1946, united various tendencies of Nigerien people in the movement for national independence. In alliance with progressive French elements and other independence African movements, the movements acquired the suppression of forced labor and arbitrary requisitions as well as legal equality between the African and the French citizens.
</p>
<h3><span id="Independence_.281958.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_(1958)">Independence (1958)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Independence (1958)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the Overseas Reform Act (<i>Loi Cadre</i>) of 23 July 1956 and the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_French_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Fifth French Republic">Fifth French Republic</a> on 4 December 1958, Niger became an autonomous state within the <a href="/wiki/French_Community" title="French Community">French Community</a>. On 18 December 1958, the Republic of Niger was officially created with <a href="/wiki/Hamani_Diori" title="Hamani Diori">Hamani Diori</a> as the head of the Counsel of Ministers of the Republic of Niger. On 11 July 1960, Niger decided to leave the French Community and acquired full independence on 3 August 1960 with Diori as its first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Niger" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of Niger">president</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F028554-0009,_Niger,_Staatsbesuch_Bundespr%C3%A4sident_L%C3%BCbke.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F028554-0009%2C_Niger%2C_Staatsbesuch_Bundespr%C3%A4sident_L%C3%BCbke.jpg/300px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F028554-0009%2C_Niger%2C_Staatsbesuch_Bundespr%C3%A4sident_L%C3%BCbke.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F028554-0009%2C_Niger%2C_Staatsbesuch_Bundespr%C3%A4sident_L%C3%BCbke.jpg/450px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F028554-0009%2C_Niger%2C_Staatsbesuch_Bundespr%C3%A4sident_L%C3%BCbke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F028554-0009%2C_Niger%2C_Staatsbesuch_Bundespr%C3%A4sident_L%C3%BCbke.jpg/600px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F028554-0009%2C_Niger%2C_Staatsbesuch_Bundespr%C3%A4sident_L%C3%BCbke.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="576" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F028554-0009,_Niger,_Staatsbesuch_Bundespr%C3%A4sident_L%C3%BCbke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/Hamani_Diori" title="Hamani Diori">Hamani Diori</a> and visiting <a href="/wiki/German_President" class="mw-redirect" title="German President">German President</a> Dr. <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_L%C3%BCbke" title="Heinrich Lübke">Heinrich Lübke</a> greet crowds on a state visit to Niamey, 1969. Diori's single party rule was characterized by good relations with the west and a preoccupation with foreign affairs.</div></div></div>
<p>For its first fourteen years as an independent state, Niger was run by a single-party civilian regime under the presidency of Diori. In 1974, a combination of devastating <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a> and accusations of rampant corruption resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d&#39;état">coup d'état</a> that overthrew the Diori regime.
</p>
<h4><span id="First_military_regime:_The_Supreme_Military_Council_and_Second_Republic_1974.E2.80.931991"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_military_regime:_The_Supreme_Military_Council_and_Second_Republic_1974–1991">First military regime: The Supreme Military Council and Second Republic 1974–1991</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: First military regime: The Supreme Military Council and Second Republic 1974–1991">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Col. <a href="/wiki/Seyni_Kountch%C3%A9" title="Seyni Kountché">Seyni Kountché</a> and a small military group under the name of Supreme Military Council deposed Diori in April 1974, following a military coup, the first of many in the post-colonial history of Niger. President Kountché ruled the country until his death in 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-Decalo1997_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Decalo1997-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first action of the Kountché military government was to address the food crisis which was one of the catalysts of the military coup.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> While political prisoners of the Diori regime were released after the coup and the country was stabilized, political and individual freedom deteriorated in general during this period. Political parties were banned. Several attempted coups (1975, 1976 and 1983) were thwarted and authors and associates were severely punished.
</p><p>Despite the restriction in freedom, the country enjoyed improved economic development with the creation of new companies, the construction of major infrastructure (building and new roads, schools, health centers) and minimal corruption in government agencies, which Kountché did not hesitate to punish severely.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> This economic development was helped by the uranium boom as well as optimal usage of public funds.
</p><p>Kountché was succeeded by his Chief of Staff, Col. <a href="/wiki/Ali_Saibou" title="Ali Saibou">Ali Saibou</a>, who was confirmed as Chief of the Supreme Military Council on 14 November 1987, four days after Kountché's death. He introduced political reforms and drafted a new constitution, with the creation of a single party. He went on to rule the country as the Chief of the Supreme Military Council.
</p><p>The 1989 referendum led to the adoption a new constitution and the creation of the Second Republic of Niger. General Saibou became the first president of the Second Republic after winning the presidential election on 10 December 1989. His presidency started during the Second Republic largely following his efforts at the end of the previous military regime with attempts at normalizing the political situation in the country with the release of <a href="/wiki/Political_prisoner" title="Political prisoner">political prisoners</a>, liberalization of laws and policies.
</p><p>President Saibou's efforts to control political reforms failed in the face of trade union and student demands to institute a <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-party democracy">multi-party democratic system</a>. On 9 February 1990, a violently repressed student march led to the death of three students,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> which led to increased national and international pressure for a National Conference. The Saibou regime acquiesced to these demands by the end of 1990.
</p>
<h4><span id="National_Conference_and_Third_Republic_1991.E2.80.931997"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Conference_and_Third_Republic_1991–1997">National Conference and Third Republic 1991–1997</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: National Conference and Third Republic 1991–1997">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The National Sovereign Conference of 1991 marked a turning point in the post-independence era of Niger and brought about multi-party democracy. From 29 July to 3 November, a national conference gathered all fringes of society to examine the political, economic and social situation of the country and make recommendations for the future direction of the country. The conference was presided over by Prof. <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Salifou" title="André Salifou">André Salifou</a> and developed a plan for a <a href="/wiki/Transitional_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitional government">transitional government</a>. This transitional government was installed in November 1991 to manage the affairs of state until the institutions of the Third Republic were put into place in April 1993.
</p><p>While the economy deteriorated over the course of the transition, there were certain notable accomplishments, including the successful conduct of a constitutional referendum; the adoption of key legislation such as the electoral and rural codes; and the holding of several free, fair, and non-violent nationwide elections. <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press" title="Freedom of the press">Freedom of the press</a> flourished, with the appearance of several new independent newspapers.
</p><p>After the National Sovereign Conference, the transitional government drafted a new constitution that eliminated the previous single-party system of the 1989 Constitution and guaranteed more freedom. The new constitution was adopted by a referendum on 26 December 1992. Following this, presidential elections were held and <a href="/wiki/Mahamane_Ousmane" title="Mahamane Ousmane">Mahamane Ousmane</a> became the first president of the Third Republic on 27 March 1993. The presidency of Mahamane Ousmane was characterized by political turbulence, with four government changes and early legislative elections called in 1995.
</p><p>The parliamentary election forced <a href="/wiki/Cohabitation_(politics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cohabitation (politics)">cohabitation</a> between a rival president and prime minister and ultimately led to governmental paralysis. As part of an initiative started under the National Sovereign Conference the government signed peace accords in April 1995 with <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tubu_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tubu people">Toubou</a> groups that had been in rebellion since 1990. These groups claimed they lacked attention and resources from the central government. The government agreed to absorb some of the former rebels into the military and, with French assistance, to help others return to a productive civilian life.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Second_military_regime.2C_Fourth_Republic.2C_third_military_regime_1997.E2.80.931999"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_military_regime,_Fourth_Republic,_third_military_regime_1997–1999">Second military regime, Fourth Republic, third military regime 1997–1999</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Second military regime, Fourth Republic, third military regime 1997–1999">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The government paralysis and the political tension was used as a motivation for a second military coup. On 27 January 1996, Col. <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Bar%C3%A9_Ma%C3%AFnassara" title="Ibrahim Baré Maïnassara">Ibrahim Baré Maïnassara</a> led a military coup that deposed President Ousmane and ended the Third Republic. Col. Maïnassara created the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Conseil_de_Salut_National&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Conseil de Salut National (page does not exist)">National Salvation Council</a> composed of military officials, which he headed. The Council carried out a six-month transition period during which a new constitution was drafted and adopted on 12 May 1996.
</p><p>Presidential campaigns were organized in the months that followed. General Maïnassara entered the campaign as an independent candidate and won the election on 8 July 1996. The elections were viewed nationally and internationally as irregular since the electoral commission was replaced during the campaign.
</p><p>Ibrahim Baré Maïnassara became the first president of the Fourth Republic. His efforts to justify questionable elections failed to convince donors to restore <a href="/wiki/Multilateralism" title="Multilateralism">multilateral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bilateralism" title="Bilateralism">bilateral</a> economic assistance; a desperate Maïnassara ignored an international <a href="/wiki/Embargo" class="mw-redirect" title="Embargo">embargo</a> against <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and sought Libyan funds to aid Niger's economy. In repeated violations of basic civil liberties by the regime, opposition leaders were imprisoned and journalists often arrested, and deported by an unofficial militia composed of police and military.
</p><p>On 9 April 1999, Maïnassara was assassinated during a military coup led by Maj. <a href="/wiki/Daouda_Malam_Wank%C3%A9" title="Daouda Malam Wanké">Daouda Malam Wanké</a>, who established a transitional National Reconciliation Council to oversee the drafting of a constitution for the Fifth Republic with a French-style <a href="/wiki/Semi-presidential_system" title="Semi-presidential system">semi-presidential system</a>. The new constitution was adopted on 9 August 1999 and was followed by presidential and legislative elections in October and November of the same year. The elections were generally found to be free and fair by international observers. Wanké withdrew himself from government affairs after the new and democratically elected president was sworn in office.
</p>
<h4><span id="Fifth_republic_1999.E2.80.932009"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fifth_republic_1999–2009">Fifth republic 1999–2009</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Fifth republic 1999–2009">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:197px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nigerien_MNJ_fighter_technical_gun.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Nigerien_MNJ_fighter_technical_gun.JPG" decoding="async" width="195" height="131" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="195" data-file-height="131" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nigerien_MNJ_fighter_technical_gun.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> rebel fighter in northern Niger, 2008</div></div></div>
<p>After winning the election in November 1999, President <a href="/wiki/Tandja_Mamadou" class="mw-redirect" title="Tandja Mamadou">Tandja Mamadou</a> was sworn in office on 22 December 1999 as the first president of the Fifth Republic. The first mandate of Tandja Mamadou brought about many administrative and economic reforms that had been halted due to the military coups since the Third Republic. In August 2002, serious unrest within military camps occurred in <a href="/wiki/Niamey" title="Niamey">Niamey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diffa" title="Diffa">Diffa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nguigmi" class="mw-redirect" title="Nguigmi">Nguigmi</a>, but the government was able to restore order within several days. On 24 July 2004, the first municipal elections in the history of Niger were held to elect local representatives, previously appointed by the government. These elections were followed by presidential elections. President Tandja Mamadou was re-elected for a second term, thus becoming the first president of the republic to win consecutive elections without being deposed by military coups. The legislative and executive configuration remained quite similar to that of the first term of the President: <a href="/wiki/Hama_Amadou" title="Hama Amadou">Hama Amadou</a> was reappointed as Prime Minister and <a href="/wiki/Mahamane_Ousmane" title="Mahamane Ousmane">Mahamane Ousmane</a>, the head of the CDS party, was re-elected as the President of the National Assembly (parliament) by his peers.
</p><p>By 2007, the relationship between President Tandja Mamadou and his prime minister had deteriorated, leading to the replacement of the latter in June 2007 by <a href="/wiki/Seyni_Oumarou" title="Seyni Oumarou">Seyni Oumarou</a> following a successful vote of no confidence at the Assembly. From 2007 to 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Tuareg_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Tuareg Rebellion">Second Tuareg Rebellion</a> took place in northern Niger, worsening economic prospects at a time of political limited progress. The political environment worsened in the following year as President Tandja Mamadou sought out to extend his presidency by modifying the constitution which limited presidential terms in Niger. Proponents of the extended presidency, rallied behind the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tazartche&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tazartche (page does not exist)">Tazartche</a> movement, were countered by opponents (anti-Tazartche) composed of opposition party militants and civil society activists.
</p>
<h4><span id="Sixth_republic_and_fourth_military_regime_2009.E2.80.932010"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sixth_republic_and_fourth_military_regime_2009–2010">Sixth republic and fourth military regime 2009–2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sixth republic and fourth military regime 2009–2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2009, President Tandja Mamadou decided to organize a constitutional referendum seeking to extend his presidency claiming to respond to the desire of the people of Niger. Despite opposition from opposition political parties and against the decision of the Constitutional Court which ruled earlier that the referendum would be unconstitutional, President Tandja Mamadou modified and adopted a new constitution by referendum. It was declared illegal by the Constitutional Court but the President dissolved the Court and assumed emergency powers. The opposition boycotted the referendum and the new constitution was adopted with 92.5% of voters and a 68% turnout, according to official results. The adoption of the new constitution created a Sixth Republic, with a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential system</a>, as well as the suspension of the 1999 Constitution and a three-year interim government with Tandja Mamadou as president. Political and social unrest spiraled before, during and after the referendum project and ultimately led to a military coup in 2010 that ended the brief existence of the 6th Republic.
</p><p>In a <a href="/wiki/2010_Nigerien_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2010 Nigerien coup d&#39;état">February 2010 coup d'état</a>, a military junta led by captain <a href="/wiki/Salou_Djibo" title="Salou Djibo">Salou Djibo</a> was established in response to Tandja's <a href="/wiki/2009%E2%80%932010_Nigerien_constitutional_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2009–2010 Nigerien constitutional crisis">attempted extension of his political term</a> by modifying the constitution. The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Council_for_the_Restoration_of_Democracy" title="Supreme Council for the Restoration of Democracy">Supreme Council for the Restoration of Democracy</a> led by General Salou Djibo carried out a one-year transition plan, drafted a new constitution and held elections in 2011 that were judged internationally as free and fair.
</p>
<h4><span id="Seventh_republic_2010.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Seventh_republic_2010–present">Seventh republic 2010–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Seventh republic 2010–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Following the adoption of the newest constitution of 2010 and the presidential elections, <a href="/wiki/Mahamadou_Issoufou" title="Mahamadou Issoufou">Mahamadou Issoufou</a> was elected as the first president of the Seventh Republic.
</p>
<h2><span id="Geography.2C_climate.2C_and_ecology"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography,_climate,_and_ecology">Geography, climate, and ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Geography, climate, and ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Niger" title="Geography of Niger">Geography of Niger</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ng-map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ng-map.png/250px-Ng-map.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/Ng-map.png 1.5x" data-file-width="329" data-file-height="352" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ng-map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of Niger</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger_sat.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Niger_sat.png/220px-Niger_sat.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Niger_sat.png/330px-Niger_sat.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Niger_sat.png/440px-Niger_sat.png 2x" data-file-width="1901" data-file-height="1421" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger_sat.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite image of Niger</div></div></div>
<p>Niger is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked" class="mw-redirect" title="Landlocked">landlocked</a> nation in West Africa located along the border between the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan</a> regions. It borders <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> to the north and <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> to the east.
</p><p>Niger lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/11th_parallel_north" title="11th parallel north">11°</a> and <a href="/wiki/24th_parallel_north" title="24th parallel north">24°N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/Prime_meridian" title="Prime meridian">0°</a> and <a href="/wiki/16th_meridian_east" title="16th meridian east">16°E</a>. Niger's area is 1,267,000 square kilometres (489,191&#160;sq&#160;mi) of which 300 square kilometres (116&#160;sq&#160;mi) is water. This makes it slightly less than twice the size of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, and the world's twenty-second largest country.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Niger borders seven countries and has a total perimeter of 5,697 kilometres (3,540&#160;mi). The longest border is with <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> to the south (1,497&#160;km or 930&#160;mi). This is followed by <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> to the east, at 1,175&#160;km (730&#160;mi), <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> to the north-northwest (956&#160;km or 594&#160;mi), and <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a> at 821&#160;km (510&#160;mi). Niger also has small borders in its far southwest with <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a> at 628&#160;km (390&#160;mi) and <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a> at 266&#160;km (165&#160;mi) and to the north-northeast <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> at 354&#160;km (220&#160;mi).
</p><p>The lowest point is the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a>, with an elevation of 200 metres (656&#160;ft). The highest point is <a href="/wiki/Mont_Idoukal-n-Tagh%C3%A8s" title="Mont Idoukal-n-Taghès">Mont Idoukal-n-Taghès</a> in the <a href="/wiki/A%C3%AFr_Mountains" title="Aïr Mountains">Aïr Mountains</a> at 2,022&#160;m (6,634&#160;ft).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Niger_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/220px-Niger_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Niger_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/330px-Niger_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Niger_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/440px-Niger_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1900" data-file-height="1900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Niger map of Köppen climate classification.</div></div></div>
<p>Niger's <a href="/wiki/Subtropical" class="mw-redirect" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> climate is mainly very hot and very dry, with much <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a> area. In the extreme south there is a tropical climate on the edges of the Niger River basin. The terrain is predominantly desert plains and <a href="/wiki/Sand_dune" class="mw-redirect" title="Sand dune">sand dunes</a>, with flat to rolling savanna in the south and hills in the north.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Niger" title="Wildlife of Niger">Wildlife of Niger</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elephant_dust_bath_park_w_niger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Elephant_dust_bath_park_w_niger.jpg/220px-Elephant_dust_bath_park_w_niger.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Elephant_dust_bath_park_w_niger.jpg/330px-Elephant_dust_bath_park_w_niger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Elephant_dust_bath_park_w_niger.jpg/440px-Elephant_dust_bath_park_w_niger.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elephant_dust_bath_park_w_niger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An elephant in the <a href="/wiki/W_National_Park" title="W National Park">W National Park</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The north of Niger is covered by large deserts and semi deserts. The typical mammal fauna consists of <a href="/wiki/Addax" title="Addax">Addax</a> antelopes, <a href="/wiki/Scimitar-horned_oryx" class="mw-redirect" title="Scimitar-horned oryx">Scimitar-horned oryx</a>, gazelles and in mountains Barbary sheep. One of the largest reserves of the world, the <a href="/wiki/A%C3%AFr_and_T%C3%A9n%C3%A9r%C3%A9_National_Nature_Reserve" title="Aïr and Ténéré National Nature Reserve">Aïr and Ténéré National Nature Reserve</a>, was founded in the northern parts of the Niger to protect these rare species.
</p><p>The southern parts of Niger are naturally dominated savannahs. The <a href="/wiki/W_National_Park" title="W National Park">W National Park</a>, situated in the bordering area to <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a>, belongs to one of the most important areas for wildlife in Western Africa, which is called the WAP (W–<a href="/wiki/Arli_National_Park" title="Arli National Park">Arli</a>–<a href="/wiki/Pendjari_National_Park" title="Pendjari National Park">Pendjari</a>) Complex. It has the most important population of the rare <a href="/wiki/West_African_lion" class="mw-redirect" title="West African lion">West African lion</a> and one of the last populations of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_African_cheetah" title="Northwest African cheetah">Northwest African cheetah</a>.
</p><p>Other wildlife includes elephants, buffaloes, <a href="/wiki/Roan_antelopes" class="mw-redirect" title="Roan antelopes">roan antelopes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kob" title="Kob">kob antelopes</a> and warthogs. The <a href="/wiki/West_African_giraffe" title="West African giraffe">West African giraffe</a> is currently not found in the W National Park, but further north in Niger, where it has its last relict population.
</p><p>Environmental issues in Niger include destructive farming practices as a result of population pressure. Illegal hunting, bush fires in some areas and human encroachment upon the flood plains of the Niger River for paddy cultivation are environmental issues. Dams constructed on the Niger River in the neighboring countries of Mali and Guinea and also within Niger itself are also cited as a reason for a reduction of water flow in the Niger River—which has a direct effect upon the environment. A lack of adequate staff to guard wildlife in the parks and reserves is another factor cited for loss of wildlife.<sup id="cite_ref-Geels2006_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geels2006-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance_and_politics">Governance and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Governance and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Niger" title="Politics of Niger">Politics of Niger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Niger" title="Government of Niger">Government of Niger</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahamadou_Issoufou-IMG_3628.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Mahamadou_Issoufou-IMG_3628.jpg/220px-Mahamadou_Issoufou-IMG_3628.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Mahamadou_Issoufou-IMG_3628.jpg/330px-Mahamadou_Issoufou-IMG_3628.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Mahamadou_Issoufou-IMG_3628.jpg/440px-Mahamadou_Issoufou-IMG_3628.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3378" data-file-height="3378" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahamadou_Issoufou-IMG_3628.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mahamadou_Issoufou" title="Mahamadou Issoufou">Mahamadou Issoufou</a>, President of Niger.</div></div></div>
<p>Niger's new constitution was approved on 31 October 2010. It restored the semi-presidential system of government of the 1999 constitution (Fifth Republic) in which the president of the republic, elected by <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a> for a five-year term, and a prime minister named by the president share <a href="/wiki/Executive_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive power">executive power</a>.
</p><p>As a reflection of Niger's increasing population, the <a href="/wiki/Unicameral" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicameral">unicameral</a> <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Niger" class="mw-redirect" title="National Assembly of Niger">National Assembly</a> was expanded in 2004 to 113 deputies elected for a five-year term under a majority system of representation. Political parties must attain at least 5 percent of the vote in order to gain a seat in the legislature.
</p><p>The constitution also provides for the popular election of municipal and local officials, and the first-ever successful municipal elections took place on 24 July 2004. The National Assembly passed in June 2002 a series of decentralization bills. As a first step, administrative powers will be distributed among 265 communes (local councils); in later stages, regions and departments will be established as decentralized entities. A new electoral code was adopted to reflect the decentralization context. The country is currently divided into 8 regions, which are subdivided into 36 districts (departments). The chief administrator (Governor) in each department is appointed by the government and functions primarily as the local agent of the central authorities.
</p><p>On 26 May 2009, President Tandja dissolved parliament after the country's constitutional court ruled against plans to hold a referendum on whether to allow him a third term in office. According to the constitution, a new parliament was elected within three months.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This began a political struggle between Tandja, trying to extend his term-limited authority beyond 2009 through the establishment of a Sixth Republic, and his opponents who demanded that he step down at the end of his second term in December 2009. See <a href="/wiki/2009_Nigerien_constitutional_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2009 Nigerien constitutional crisis">2009 Nigerien constitutional crisis</a>. The military took over the country and President Tandja was put in prison, charged with corruption.
</p><p>The military kept their promise to return the country to democratic civilian rule. A constitutional referendum and national elections were held. A presidential election was held on 31 January 2011, but as no clear winner emerged, run-off elections were held on 12 March 2011. <a href="/wiki/Mahamadou_Issoufou" title="Mahamadou Issoufou">Mahamadou Issoufou</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Nigerien_Party_for_Democracy_and_Socialism" title="Nigerien Party for Democracy and Socialism">Nigerien Party for Democracy and Socialism</a> was elected president. A parliamentary election was held at the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Drapeau_Niger_ambassade_Paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Drapeau_Niger_ambassade_Paris.jpg/200px-Drapeau_Niger_ambassade_Paris.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Drapeau_Niger_ambassade_Paris.jpg/300px-Drapeau_Niger_ambassade_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Drapeau_Niger_ambassade_Paris.jpg/400px-Drapeau_Niger_ambassade_Paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1738" data-file-height="2310" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Drapeau_Niger_ambassade_Paris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Niger's flag waving at the embassy in Paris.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Niger" title="Foreign relations of Niger">Foreign relations of Niger</a></div>
<p>Niger pursues a moderate foreign policy and maintains friendly relations with the West and the Islamic world as well as non-aligned countries. It belongs to the UN and its main specialized agencies and in 1980–81 served on the <a href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Security Council">UN Security Council</a>. Niger maintains a special relationship with former colonial power France and has close relations with its West African neighbors.
</p><p>It is a charter member of the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/West_African_Monetary_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="West African Monetary Union">West African Monetary Union</a> and also belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Niger_Basin_Authority" title="Niger Basin Authority">Niger Basin Authority</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad_Basin_Commission" title="Lake Chad Basin Commission">Lake Chad Basin Commission</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">Economic Community of West African States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_the_Harmonization_of_Business_Law_in_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization for the Harmonization of Business Law in Africa">Organization for the Harmonization of Business Law in Africa</a> (<a href="/wiki/OHADA" title="OHADA">OHADA</a>). The westernmost regions of Niger are joined with contiguous regions of Mali and Burkina Faso under the <a href="/wiki/Liptako-Gourma_Authority" title="Liptako-Gourma Authority">Liptako-Gourma Authority</a>.
</p><p>The border dispute with Benin, inherited from colonial times and concerning inter alia <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9t%C3%A9_Island" title="Lété Island">Lété Island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a>, was solved by the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> in 2005 to Niger's advantage.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_finance">Government finance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Government finance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Government finance is derived revenue exports (Mining, oil and agricultural exports) as well as various forms of taxes collected by the government. In the past, foreign aid has contributed to large percentages of the budget. In 2013, Niger's government has adopted a zero-deficit budget of 1.279 trillion CFA francs ($2.53 billion) which is claimed to balance revenues and expenditures by an 11% reduction in the budget from the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2014 budget was 1.867 trillion CFA which is distributed as follows according to: public debt (76,703,692,000 CFA), personnel expenditures (210,979,633,960 CFA), operating expenditures (128,988,777,711 CFA); subsidies and transfers: 308,379,641,366 CFA) and Investment (1,142,513,658,712 CFA).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_aid">Foreign aid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Foreign aid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The importance of external support for Niger's development is demonstrated by the fact that about 45% of the government's FY 2002 budget, including 80% of its capital budget, derives from donor resources.<sup id="cite_ref-ussd2009_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ussd2009-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The most important donors in Niger are France, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>, and various <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> agencies (<a href="/wiki/UNDP" class="mw-redirect" title="UNDP">UNDP</a>, <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a>, <a href="/wiki/FAO" class="mw-redirect" title="FAO">FAO</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="World Food Program">World Food Program</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Fund" title="United Nations Population Fund">United Nations Population Fund</a>).
</p><p>Other principal donors include the United States, Belgium, Germany, Switzerland, Canada, and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. While <a href="/wiki/USAID" class="mw-redirect" title="USAID">USAID</a> does not have an office in Niger, the United States is a major donor, contributing nearly $10 million each year to Niger's development. The U.S. also is a major partner in policy coordination in such areas as food security and HIV/AIDS.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_system">Judicial system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Judicial system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_of_Niger" title="Judiciary of Niger">Judiciary of Niger</a></div>
<p>The current Judiciary of Niger was established with the creation of the Fourth Republic in 1999. The constitution of December 1992 was revised by national referendum on 12 May 1996 and, again, by referendum, revised to the current version on 18 July 1999. It is based on the <a href="/wiki/Code_Napoleon" class="mw-redirect" title="Code Napoleon">Code Napoleon</a> "<i><a href="/wiki/Inquisitorial_system" title="Inquisitorial system">Inquisitorial system</a></i>", established in Niger during French colonial rule and the 1960 Constitution of Niger. The Court of Appeals reviews questions of fact and law, while the Supreme Court reviews application of the law and constitutional questions. The High Court of Justice (HCJ) deals with cases involving senior government officials. The justice system also includes civil criminal courts, customary courts, traditional mediation, and a military court.<sup id="cite_ref-Sory_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sory-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The military court provides the same rights as civil criminal courts; however, customary courts do not. The military court cannot try civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-USHRR2008_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USHRR2008-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_enforcement">Law enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Law enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Niger" title="Law enforcement in Niger">Law enforcement in Niger</a></div>
<p>Law enforcement in Niger is the responsibility of the Ministry of Defense through the <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie_Nationale_(Niger)" title="Gendarmerie Nationale (Niger)">National Gendarmerie</a> and the Ministry of the Interior through the <a href="/wiki/National_Police_(Niger)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Police (Niger)">National Police</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Guard_of_Niger" title="National Guard of Niger">National Guard</a>. The <a href="/wiki/National_Police_(Niger)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Police (Niger)">National Police</a> is primarily responsible for law enforcement in urban areas. Outside big cities and in rural areas, this responsibility falls on the <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie_Nationale_(Niger)" title="Gendarmerie Nationale (Niger)">National Gendarmerie</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Guard_of_Niger" title="National Guard of Niger">National Guard</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Niger_Armed_Forces" title="Niger Armed Forces">Niger Armed Forces</a></div>
<p>The Niger Armed Forces (Forces armées nigériennes) are the military and paramilitary forces of Niger, under the president as supreme commander. They consist of the Niger Army (Armée de Terre), the Niger Air Force (Armée de l'Air) and the auxiliary paramilitary forces, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie_Nationale_(Niger)" title="Gendarmerie Nationale (Niger)">National Gendarmerie (Gendarmerie nationale)</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Guard_of_Niger" title="National Guard of Niger">National Guard (Garde Nationale)</a>. Both paramilitary forces are trained in military fashion and have some military responsibilities in wartime. In peace time their duties are mostly policing duties.
</p><p>The armed forces are composed of approximately 12,900 personnel, including 3,700 <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie_Nationale_(Niger)" title="Gendarmerie Nationale (Niger)">gendarmes</a>, 3200 <a href="/wiki/National_Guard_of_Niger" title="National Guard of Niger">national guards</a>, 300 air force personnel, and 6,000 army personnel. The armed forces of Niger have been involved several military coups over the years with the most recent in 2010. Niger's armed forces have a long history of military cooperation with France and the United States. As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Niamey is home to a U.S. drone base.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger_administrative_divisions.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Niger_administrative_divisions.svg/500px-Niger_administrative_divisions.svg.png" decoding="async" width="500" height="353" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Niger_administrative_divisions.svg/750px-Niger_administrative_divisions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Niger_administrative_divisions.svg/1000px-Niger_administrative_divisions.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1123" data-file-height="793" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger_administrative_divisions.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Administrative divisions of Niger</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Niger" title="Regions of Niger">Regions of Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_Niger" title="Departments of Niger">Departments of Niger</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Communes_of_Niger" title="Communes of Niger">Communes of Niger</a></div>
<p>Niger is divided into 7 <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Niger" title="Regions of Niger">Regions</a> and one capital district. These Regions are subdivided into 36 <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_Niger" title="Departments of Niger">departments</a>. The 36 Departments are currently broken down into Communes of varying types. As of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> there were 265 communes, including communes urbaines (Urban Communes: as subdivisions of major cities), communes rurales (Rural Communes), in sparsely populated areas and postes administratifs (Administrative Posts) for largely uninhabited desert areas or military zones.
</p><p>Rural communes may contain official villages and settlements, while Urban Communes are divided into quarters. Niger subvisions were renamed in 2002, in the implementation of a decentralisation project, first begun in 1998. Previously, Niger was divided into 7 Departments, 36 Arrondissements, and Communes. These subdivisions were administered by officials appointed by the national government. These offices will be replaced in the future by democratically elected councils at each level.
</p><p>The <i>pre-2002</i> departments (renamed as regions) and capital district are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Agadez_Region" title="Agadez Region">Agadez Region</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Diffa_Region" title="Diffa Region">Diffa Region</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dosso_Region" title="Dosso Region">Dosso Region</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maradi_Region" title="Maradi Region">Maradi Region</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tahoua_Region" title="Tahoua Region">Tahoua Region</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tillab%C3%A9ri_Region" title="Tillabéri Region">Tillabéri Region</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zinder_Region" title="Zinder Region">Zinder Region</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Niamey" title="Niamey">Niamey</a> <span style="font-size:90%;">(capital district)</span></li></ul>
