<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Niger" title="Economy of Niger">Economy of Niger</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niamey_night.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Niamey_night.jpg/220px-Niamey_night.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Niamey_night.jpg/330px-Niamey_night.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Niamey_night.jpg/440px-Niamey_night.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niamey_night.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Niamey" title="Niamey">Niamey</a>, Niger's capital and economic hub.</div></div></div>
<p>The economy of Niger centers on subsistence crops, livestock, and some of the world's largest uranium deposits. Drought cycles, desertification, a 2.9% population growth rate, and the drop in world demand for uranium have undercut the economy.
</p><p>Niger shares a common currency, the <a href="/wiki/CFA_franc" title="CFA franc">CFA franc</a>, and a common central bank, the <a href="/wiki/Central_Bank_of_West_African_States" title="Central Bank of West African States">Central Bank of West African States</a> (BCEAO), with seven other members of the <a href="/wiki/West_African_Monetary_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="West African Monetary Union">West African Monetary Union</a>. Niger is also a member of the <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_the_Harmonization_of_Business_Law_in_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization for the Harmonization of Business Law in Africa">Organization for the Harmonization of Business Law in Africa</a> (OHADA).<sup id="cite_ref-ohada.com_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohada.com-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2000, Niger qualified for enhanced debt relief under the International Monetary Fund program for <a href="/wiki/Heavily_Indebted_Poor_Countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavily Indebted Poor Countries">Heavily Indebted Poor Countries</a> (HIPC) and concluded an agreement with the Fund for <a href="/wiki/Poverty_Reduction_and_Growth_Facility" title="Poverty Reduction and Growth Facility">Poverty Reduction and Growth Facility</a> (PRGF). Debt relief provided under the enhanced HIPC initiative significantly reduces Niger's annual debt service obligations, freeing funds for expenditures on basic health care, primary education, HIV/AIDS prevention, rural infrastructure, and other programs geared at poverty reduction.
</p><p>In December 2005, it was announced that Niger had received 100% multilateral <a href="/wiki/Debt_relief" title="Debt relief">debt relief</a> from the <a href="/wiki/IMF" class="mw-redirect" title="IMF">IMF</a>, which translates into the forgiveness of approximately US$86&#160;million in debts to the IMF, excluding the remaining assistance under HIPC. Nearly half of the government's budget is derived from foreign donor resources. Future growth may be sustained by exploitation of oil, gold, coal, and other mineral resources. Uranium prices have recovered somewhat in the last few years. A drought and locust infestation in 2005 led to food shortages for as many as 2.5&#160;million Nigeriens.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Niger" title="Demographics of Niger">Demographics of Niger</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1997_275-15_young_Wodaabe_women.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/1997_275-15_young_Wodaabe_women.jpg/220px-1997_275-15_young_Wodaabe_women.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/1997_275-15_young_Wodaabe_women.jpg/330px-1997_275-15_young_Wodaabe_women.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/1997_275-15_young_Wodaabe_women.jpg/440px-1997_275-15_young_Wodaabe_women.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1151" data-file-height="995" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1997_275-15_young_Wodaabe_women.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fulani</a> women with traditional <a href="/wiki/Face_tattoo" title="Face tattoo">facial tattoos</a>.</div></div></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left: 10px">

<td style="text-align:right;">20.7

<p>As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the population of Niger was 20,672,987<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>. Expanding from a population of 3.4 million in 1960, Niger's population has rapidly increased with a current growth rate of 3.3% (7.1 children per mother<sup id="cite_ref-ins-demographics_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ins-demographics-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This growth rate is one of the highest in the world and is a source of concern for the government and international agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-irin-demographics_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irin-demographics-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The population is predominantly young, with 49.2% under 15 years old and 2.7% over 65 years, and predominantly rural with only 21% living in urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-ins-demographics_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ins-demographics-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2005 study stated that over 800,000 people (nearly 8% of the population) <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Niger" title="Slavery in Niger">in Niger are enslaved</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zarma_people" title="Zarma people">Zarma people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fula people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tubu_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tubu people">Tubu people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diffa_Arabs" title="Diffa Arabs">Diffa Arabs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gurma_people" title="Gurma people">Gurma people</a></div>
<p>Niger has a wide variety of ethnic groups as in most West African countries. The ethnic makeup of Niger is as follows: <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a> (53.0%), <a href="/wiki/Zarma_people" title="Zarma people">Zarma-Songhai</a> (21.2%), <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> (10.4%), <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fula</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Peuls</i>; <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Fula</a>: <i lang="ff-Latn">Fulɓe</i>) (9.9%), <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri Manga</a> (4.4%), <a href="/wiki/Tubu_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tubu people">Tubu</a> (0.4%), <a href="/wiki/Diffa_Arabs" title="Diffa Arabs">Arab</a> (0.3%), <a href="/wiki/Gurma_people" title="Gurma people">Gourmantche</a> (0.3%), other (0.2%).<sup id="cite_ref-ins-demographics_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ins-demographics-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Niger" title="Languages of Niger">Languages of Niger</a></div>
<p>French, inherited from the colonial period, is the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>. It is spoken mainly as a second language by people who have received a formal western education and serves as the administrative language. Niger has been a member of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_Internationale_de_la_Francophonie" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation Internationale de la Francophonie">Organisation Internationale de la Francophonie</a> since 1970.
</p><p>Niger has ten recognized <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national languages</a>, namely <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buduma_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Buduma language">Buduma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Fulfulde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gurma_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurma language">Gourmanchéma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">Hausa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_language" title="Kanuri language">Kanuri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Songhay_languages" title="Songhay languages">Zarma &amp; Songhai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_languages" title="Tuareg languages">Tamasheq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tasawaq_language" title="Tasawaq language">Tassawaq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tebu_languages" title="Tebu languages">Tebu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-axl.cefan.ulaval.ca_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-axl.cefan.ulaval.ca-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Each is spoken as a first language primarily by the ethnic group with which it is associated.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Hausa and Zarma-Sonrai, the two most spoken languages, are widely spoken throughout the country as first or second languages.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Largest_cities">Largest cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Largest cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Niger" title="Religion in Niger">Religion in Niger</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niamey_Mosque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Niamey_Mosque.jpg/220px-Niamey_Mosque.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Niamey_Mosque.jpg/330px-Niamey_Mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Niamey_Mosque.jpg/440px-Niamey_Mosque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2940" data-file-height="1972" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niamey_Mosque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mosque in <a href="/wiki/Niamey" title="Niamey">Niamey</a></div></div></div>
<div class="barbox tright" style="overflow-x: auto;">
<div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:auto; background: white;">
<table style="text-align:left; border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">
<tbody><tr style="background:#ddd"><th style="text-align:center;" colspan="5">Religion in Niger</th></tr>
<tr style="font-size:88%; height:4px;">
<td style="padding:0 4px; text-align:left;">religion</td>
<td style="padding:0 4px; text-align:right;"></td>
<td style="width:100px; text-align:left;"></td>
<td style="padding:0 4px; width:1em; text-align:right;">percent<sup id="cite_ref-census_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup></td>
<td style="padding:0 4px; text-align:right;"></td>
</tr>
<tr>

<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">Christianity</td>

<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;"><div style="background:blue; width:0.2%; overflow: hidden;">&#8201;</div></td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">0.2%</td>

<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">Irreligious</td>
<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;"><div style="background:black; width:0.1%; overflow: hidden;">&#8201;</div></td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">0.1%</td>
</tr>

</tbody></table>
</div>
</div>
<p>Niger is a <a href="/wiki/Secular_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Secular country">secular country</a> and separation of state and religion is guaranteed by Articles 3 and 175 of the 2010 Constitution, which dictate that future amendments or revisions may not modify the secular nature of the republic of Niger. <a href="/wiki/Religious_freedom" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious freedom">Religious freedom</a> is protected by Article 30 of the same constitution. <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, widespread in the region since the 10th century, has greatly shaped the culture and mores of the people of Niger. Islam is the most dominant religion, practiced by 99.3% of the population according to the 2012 census.<sup id="cite_ref-census_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The other two main religions of Niger are <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, practiced by 0.3% of the population, and Animism (<a href="/wiki/African_traditional_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="African traditional religion">traditional indigenous religious beliefs</a>), practiced by 0.2% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-census_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Christianity was established earlier in the country by missionaries during the French colonial years. Other urban Christian expatriate communities from Europe and West Africa are also presented. Religious persecution is rare in Niger which is ranked last (#50) on the <a href="/wiki/World_Watch_List" class="mw-redirect" title="World Watch List">World Watch List</a> for severity of persecution that Christians face for actively pursuing their faith.
</p><p>The numbers of Animist practitioners are a point of contention. As recently as the late 19th century, much of the south center of the nation was unreached by Islam, and the conversion of some rural areas has been only partial. There are still areas where animist based festivals and traditions (such as the <a href="/wiki/Bori_(religion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bori (religion)">Bori religion</a>) are practiced by <a href="/wiki/Syncretic" class="mw-redirect" title="Syncretic">syncretic</a> Muslim communities (in some Hausa areas as well as among some <a href="/wiki/Toubou" class="mw-redirect" title="Toubou">Toubou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wodaabe" title="Wodaabe">Wodaabe</a> pastoralists), as opposed to several small communities who maintain their pre-Islamic religion. These include the Hausa-speaking <a href="/wiki/Maouri_people" title="Maouri people">Maouri</a> (or <i>Azna</i>, the Hausa word for "pagan") community in <a href="/wiki/Dogondoutci" class="mw-redirect" title="Dogondoutci">Dogondoutci</a> in the south-southwest and the <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_language" title="Kanuri language">Kanuri</a> speaking Manga near <a href="/wiki/Zinder" title="Zinder">Zinder</a>, both of whom practice variations of the pre-Islamic Hausa <a href="/wiki/Maguzawa" class="mw-redirect" title="Maguzawa">Maguzawa</a> religion. There are also some tiny Boudouma and Songhay animist communities in the southwest.<sup id="cite_ref-Decalo79_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Decalo79-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Niger" title="Islam in Niger">Islam in Niger</a></div>
<p>The majority of Muslims in Niger are <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a>, 7% are <a href="/wiki/Shi%27a" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi&#39;a">Shi'a</a>, 5% are <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a> and 20% <a href="/wiki/Non-denominational" title="Non-denominational">non-denominational</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pew_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-report_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Islam was spread into what is now Niger beginning in the 15th century, by both the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai Empire</a> in the west, and the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">Trans-Saharan trade</a> traveling from the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> expansion from the north, culminating in their seizure of the far eastern oases from the <a href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Bornu Empire">Kanem-Bornu Empire</a> in the 17th centuries, spread distinctively <a href="/wiki/Berber_mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Berber mythology">Berber</a> practices.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger,_Filingu%C3%A9_(25),_street_scene_with_mosque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Niger%2C_Filingu%C3%A9_%2825%29%2C_street_scene_with_mosque.jpg/220px-Niger%2C_Filingu%C3%A9_%2825%29%2C_street_scene_with_mosque.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="86" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Niger%2C_Filingu%C3%A9_%2825%29%2C_street_scene_with_mosque.jpg/330px-Niger%2C_Filingu%C3%A9_%2825%29%2C_street_scene_with_mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Niger%2C_Filingu%C3%A9_%2825%29%2C_street_scene_with_mosque.jpg/440px-Niger%2C_Filingu%C3%A9_%2825%29%2C_street_scene_with_mosque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4356" data-file-height="1704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger,_Filingu%C3%A9_(25),_street_scene_with_mosque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Small mosque in <a href="/wiki/Filingue" title="Filingue">Filingue</a></div></div></div>
<p>Both <a href="/wiki/Zarma_people" title="Zarma people">Zarma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a> areas were greatly influenced by the 18th- and 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Fula_jihads" title="Fula jihads">Fula led</a> <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> brotherhoods, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Caliphate" title="Sokoto Caliphate">Sokoto Caliphate</a> (in today's Nigeria). Modern Muslim practice in Niger is often tied to the <a href="/wiki/Tijaniya" class="mw-redirect" title="Tijaniya">Tijaniya</a> <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> <a href="/wiki/Tariqah" class="mw-redirect" title="Tariqah">brotherhoods</a>, although there are small minority groups tied to <a href="/wiki/Hammallism" class="mw-redirect" title="Hammallism">Hammallism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nyassist" class="mw-redirect" title="Nyassist">Nyassist</a> Sufi orders in the west, and the <a href="/wiki/Sanusiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanusiya">Sanusiya</a> in the far northeast.<sup id="cite_ref-Decalo79_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Decalo79-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A small center of followers of Salafi movement within Sunni Islam have appeared in the last thirty years, in the capital and in <a href="/wiki/Maradi,_Niger" title="Maradi, Niger">Maradi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> These small groups, linked to similar groups in <a href="/wiki/Jos" title="Jos">Jos</a>, Nigeria, came to public prominence in the 1990s during a series of religious riots.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite this, Niger maintains a tradition as a <a href="/wiki/Secular_state" title="Secular state">secular state</a>, protected by law.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Interfaith relations are deemed very good, and the forms of Islam traditionally practiced in most of the country are marked by tolerance of other faiths and lack of restrictions on personal freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Divorce and <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">polygyny</a> are unremarkable, women are not secluded, and head coverings are not mandatory—they are often a rarity in urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Alcohol, such as the locally produced Bière Niger, is sold openly in most of the country.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Niger" title="Education in Niger">Education in Niger</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger_primary_school_MCC3500.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Niger_primary_school_MCC3500.jpg/220px-Niger_primary_school_MCC3500.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Niger_primary_school_MCC3500.jpg/330px-Niger_primary_school_MCC3500.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Niger_primary_school_MCC3500.jpg/440px-Niger_primary_school_MCC3500.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="289" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niger_primary_school_MCC3500.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A primary classroom in Niger.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Literacy_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Literacy rate">literacy rate</a> of Niger is among the lowest in the world; in 2005 it was estimated to be only 28.7% (42.9% male and 15.1% female).<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Primary education in Niger is compulsory for six years.<sup id="cite_ref-ilab_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilab-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The primary school enrollment and attendance rates are low, particularly for girls.<sup id="cite_ref-ilab_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilab-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In 1997, the gross primary enrollment rate was 29.3 percent, and in 1996, the net primary enrollment rate was 24.5 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-ilab_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilab-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>About 60 percent of children who finish primary schools are boys, as the majority of girls rarely attend school for more than a few years.<sup id="cite_ref-ilab_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilab-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Children are often forced to work rather than attend school, particularly during planting or <a href="/wiki/Harvest" title="Harvest">harvest</a> periods.<sup id="cite_ref-ilab_63-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilab-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">Nomadic</a> children in the north of the country often do not have access to schools.<sup id="cite_ref-ilab_63-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilab-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Niger" title="Health in Niger">Health in Niger</a></div>
<p>The child mortality rate in Niger (deaths among children between the ages of 1 and 4) is high (248 per 1,000) due to generally poor health conditions and inadequate nutrition for most of the country's children. According to the organization <a href="/wiki/Save_the_Children" title="Save the Children">Save the Children</a>, Niger has the world's highest <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Infant mortality rate">infant mortality rate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Niger also has the highest <a href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertility rate">fertility rate</a> in the world (6.49 births per woman according to 2017 estimates<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>); this means that nearly half (49%) of the Nigerien population is under age 15. Niger has the 11th highest <a href="/wiki/Maternal_mortality" class="mw-redirect" title="Maternal mortality">maternal mortality</a> rate in the world at 820 deaths/100,000 live births.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> There were 3 physicians and 22 nurses per 100,000 persons in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Niger" title="Culture of Niger">Culture of Niger</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zinder_sultans_horsemen_festival.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Zinder_sultans_horsemen_festival.jpg/220px-Zinder_sultans_horsemen_festival.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Zinder_sultans_horsemen_festival.jpg/330px-Zinder_sultans_horsemen_festival.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Zinder_sultans_horsemen_festival.jpg/440px-Zinder_sultans_horsemen_festival.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zinder_sultans_horsemen_festival.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Horsemen at the traditional <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a> festival at the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Damagaram" title="Sultanate of Damagaram">Sultan's</a> Palace in the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Zinder" title="Zinder">Zinder</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_home_zinder_niger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ancient_home_zinder_niger.jpg/220px-Ancient_home_zinder_niger.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ancient_home_zinder_niger.jpg/330px-Ancient_home_zinder_niger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ancient_home_zinder_niger.jpg/440px-Ancient_home_zinder_niger.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_home_zinder_niger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A traditional home in <a href="/wiki/Zinder" title="Zinder">Zinder</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Nigerien culture is marked by variation, evidence of the cultural crossroads which <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empires" class="mw-redirect" title="French colonial empires">French colonialism</a> formed into a unified state from the beginning of the 20th century. What is now Niger was created from four distinct cultural areas in the pre-colonial era: the <a href="/wiki/Zarma_people" title="Zarma people">Zarma</a> dominated <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a> valley in the southwest; the northern periphery of <a href="/wiki/Hausaland" class="mw-redirect" title="Hausaland">Hausaland</a>, made mostly of those states which had resisted the <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Caliphate" title="Sokoto Caliphate">Sokoto Caliphate</a>, and ranged along the long southern border with Nigeria; the <a href="/wiki/Chad_basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Chad basin">Lake Chad basin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kaouar" title="Kaouar">Kaouar</a> in the far east, populated by <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri</a> farmers and <a href="/wiki/Toubou" class="mw-redirect" title="Toubou">Toubou</a> pastoralists who had once been part of the <a href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Bornu Empire">Kanem-Bornu Empire</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> nomads of the <a href="/wiki/A%C3%AFr_Mountains" title="Aïr Mountains">Aïr Mountains</a> and Saharan desert in the vast north.
</p><p>Each of these communities, along with smaller ethnic groups like the pastoral <a href="/wiki/Wodaabe" title="Wodaabe">Wodaabe</a> <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fula</a>, brought their own cultural traditions to the new state of Niger. While successive post-independence governments have tried to forge a shared national culture, this has been slow forming, in part because the major Nigerien communities have their own cultural histories, and in part because Nigerien ethnic groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a>, Tuareg and Kanuri are but part of larger ethnic communities which cross borders introduced under colonialism.
</p><p>Until the 1990s, government and politics was inordinately dominated by <a href="/wiki/Niamey" title="Niamey">Niamey</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Zarma_people" title="Zarma people">Zarma people</a> of the surrounding region. At the same time the plurality of the population, in the Hausa borderlands between <a href="/wiki/Birni-N%27Konni" title="Birni-N&#39;Konni">Birni-N'Konni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maine-Soroa" class="mw-redirect" title="Maine-Soroa">Maine-Soroa</a>, have often looked culturally more to Hausaland in Nigeria than Niamey. Between 1996 and 2003, primary school attendance was around 30%,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> including 36% of males and only 25% of females. Additional education occurs through <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrasas</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals_and_cultural_events">Festivals and cultural events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Festivals and cultural events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Gu.C3.A9rewol_festival"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Guérewol_festival">Guérewol festival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Guérewol festival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gu%C3%A9rewol" title="Guérewol">Guérewol</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1997_274-5_Gerewol.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/1997_274-5_Gerewol.jpg/200px-1997_274-5_Gerewol.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/1997_274-5_Gerewol.jpg/300px-1997_274-5_Gerewol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/1997_274-5_Gerewol.jpg/400px-1997_274-5_Gerewol.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1043" data-file-height="1560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1997_274-5_Gerewol.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Participants in the <a href="/wiki/Gu%C3%A9rewol" title="Guérewol">Guérewol</a> perform the <i>Guérewol</i> dance, 1997.</div></div></div>
<p>The Guérewol festival is a traditional Wodaabe cultural event that takes place in <a href="/wiki/Abalak" title="Abalak">Abalak</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tahoua_Region" title="Tahoua Region">Tahoua region</a> or <a href="/wiki/In-Gall" title="In-Gall">In'Gall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Agadez_Region" title="Agadez Region">Agadez Region</a>. It is an annual traditional courtship ritual practiced by the Wodaabe (Fula) people of Niger. During this ceremony, young men dressed in elaborate ornamentation and made up in traditional face painting gather in lines to dance and sing, vying for the attention of marriageable young women. The Guérewol festival is an international attraction and was featured in films and magazines as prominent as the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Geographic (magazine)">National Geographic</a>.
</p>
<h4><span id="Cure_Sal.C3.A9e_festival"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cure_Salée_festival">Cure Salée festival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Cure Salée festival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cure_Salee" title="Cure Salee">Cure Salee</a></div>
<p>"La Cure salée" (English: Salt Cure) is a yearly festival of Tuareg and Wodaabe nomads in <a href="/wiki/In-Gall" title="In-Gall">In'Gall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Agadez_Region" title="Agadez Region">Agadez Region</a> traditionally to celebrate the end of the rainy season. For three days, the festival features a parade of <a href="/wiki/Tuareg" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuareg">Tuareg</a> camel riders followed with camel and horse races, songs, dances, and storytelling.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Niger" title="Media of Niger">Media of Niger</a></div>
<p>Niger began developing diverse media in the late 1990s. Prior to the Third Republic, Nigeriens only had access to tightly controlled state media.<sup id="cite_ref-sainforeport2002fr_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sainforeport2002fr-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Now Niamey contains scores of newspapers and magazines; some, like <i>Le Sahel</i>, are government operated, while many are critical of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Radio is the most important medium, as television sets are beyond the buying power of many of the rural poor, and illiteracy prevents print media from becoming a mass medium.<sup id="cite_ref-Geels2006_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geels2006-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the national and regional radio services of the state broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Radio_and_Television_of_Niger" title="Office of Radio and Television of Niger">ORTN</a>, there are four privately owned radio networks which total more than 100 stations. Three of them—the <a href="/wiki/Anfani_FM" title="Anfani FM">Anfani Group</a>, Sarounia and Tenere—are urban-based commercial-format <a href="/wiki/FM_broadcasting" title="FM broadcasting">FM</a> networks in the major towns.<sup id="cite_ref-ussd9506_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ussd9506-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> There is also a network of over 80 community radio stations spread across all seven regions of the country, governed by the Comité de Pilotage de Radios de Proximité (CPRP), a civil society organisation. The independent-sector radio networks are collectively estimated by CPRP officials to cover some 7.6&#160;million people, or about 73% of the population (2005).
</p><p>Aside from Nigerien radio stations, the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>'s Hausa service is listened to on FM repeaters across wide parts of the country, particularly in the south, close to the border with Nigeria. <a href="/wiki/Radio_France_Internationale" title="Radio France Internationale">Radio France Internationale</a> also rebroadcasts in French through some of the commercial stations, via satellite. Tenere FM also runs a national independent television station of the same name.<sup id="cite_ref-ussd9506_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ussd9506-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite relative freedom at the national level, Nigerien <a href="/wiki/Journalism" title="Journalism">journalists</a> say they are often pressured by local authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-issa04_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-issa04-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The state ORTN network depends financially on the government, partly through a surcharge on electricity bills, and partly through direct subsidy. The sector is governed by the <a href="/wiki/High_Council_for_Communication_(Niger)" title="High Council for Communication (Niger)">Conseil Supérieur de Communications</a>, established as an independent body in the early 1990s, since 2007 headed by Daouda Diallo. International human rights groups have criticised the government since at least 1996 as using regulation and police to punish criticism of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-cpj2006_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cpj2006-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

Committee to Protect Journalists (2007). Retrieved 23 February 2009.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://asiapacific.amnesty.org/library/Index/ENGAFR430012007?open&amp;of=ENG-2F5">Niger: Emergency legislation infringes non-derogable human rights</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090211134003/http://asiapacific.amnesty.org/library/Index/ENGAFR430012007?open&amp;of=ENG-2F5">Archived</a> 11 February 2009 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.
